<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Production">Production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Development_and_pre-production">Development and pre-production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Development and pre-production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Development began when <a href="/wiki/Walt_Disney" title="Walt Disney">Walt Disney</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Snow_White_and_the_Seven_Dwarfs_(1937_film)" title="Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs (1937 film)">Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</a></i> (1937) showed that films adapted from popular children's stories and fairytale folklore could be successful.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fricke2_12-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fricke2-12">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic-13">[13]</a></sup> In January 1938, <a href="/wiki/Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer" title="Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer">Metro-Goldwyn-Mayer</a> bought the rights to the <a href="/wiki/The_Wonderful_Wizard_of_Oz" title="The Wonderful Wizard of Oz">hugely popular novel</a> from <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Goldwyn" title="Samuel Goldwyn">Samuel Goldwyn</a>, who had toyed with the idea of making the film as a vehicle for <a href="/wiki/Eddie_Cantor" title="Eddie Cantor">Eddie Cantor</a>, who was under contract to the Goldwyn studios and whom Goldwyn wanted to cast as the Scarecrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic_13-1"><a href="#cite_note-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>The script went through a number of writers and revisions before the final shooting.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmetz_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harmetz-14">[14]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Mervyn_LeRoy" title="Mervyn LeRoy">Mervyn LeRoy</a>'s assistant William H. Cannon had submitted a brief four-page outline.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmetz_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-Harmetz-14">[14]</a></sup> Because recent fantasy films had not fared well, he recommended that the magical elements of the story be toned down or eliminated. In his outline, the Scarecrow was a man so stupid that the only employment open to him was literally scaring crows from cornfields, and the Tin Woodman was a criminal so heartless he was sentenced to be placed in a tin suit for eternity; this torture softened him into someone gentle and kind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmetz_14-2"><a href="#cite_note-Harmetz-14">[14]</a></sup> His vision was similar to <a href="/wiki/Larry_Semon" title="Larry Semon">Larry Semon</a>'s <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wizard_of_Oz_(1925_film)" title="Wizard of Oz (1925 film)">1925 film adaptation</a> of the story, in which the magical element is absent.</p>
<p>After that, LeRoy hired screenwriter <a href="/wiki/Herman_J._Mankiewicz" title="Herman J. Mankiewicz">Herman J. Mankiewicz</a>, who soon delivered a 17-page draft of the Kansas scenes, and a few weeks later, a further 56 pages. <a href="/wiki/Noel_Langley" title="Noel Langley">Noel Langley</a> and poet <a href="/wiki/Ogden_Nash" title="Ogden Nash">Ogden Nash</a> were also hired to write separate versions of the story. None of these three knew about the others, and this was not an uncommon procedure. Nash delivered a four-page outline, Langley turned in a 43-page treatment and a full film script. He<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag possibly uses too-vague attribution or weasel words. (August 2017)">who?</span></a></i>]</sup> turned in three more, this time incorporating the songs that had been written by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Arlen" title="Harold Arlen">Harold Arlen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Yip_Harburg" title="Yip Harburg">Yip Harburg</a>. <a href="/wiki/Florence_Ryerson" title="Florence Ryerson">Florence Ryerson</a> and <a href="/wiki/Edgar_Allan_Woolf" title="Edgar Allan Woolf">Edgar Allan Woolf</a> submitted a script and were brought on board to touch up the writing. They would be responsible for making sure the story stayed true to the Baum book. However, producer <a href="/wiki/Arthur_Freed" title="Arthur Freed">Arthur Freed</a> was unhappy with their work and reassigned it to Langley.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Coan_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Coan-15">[15]</a></sup> During filming, <a href="/wiki/Victor_Fleming" title="Victor Fleming">Victor Fleming</a> and <a href="/wiki/John_Lee_Mahin" title="John Lee Mahin">John Lee Mahin</a> revised the script further, adding and cutting some scenes. In addition, Jack Haley and Bert Lahr are known to have written some of their own dialogue for the Kansas sequence.</p>
<p>The final draft of the script was completed on October 8, 1938, following numerous rewrites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-16">[16]</a></sup> All in all, it was a mish-mash of many creative minds, but Langley, Ryerson, and Woolf got the film credits. Along with the contributors already mentioned, others who assisted with the adaptation without receiving credit include: <a href="/wiki/Irving_Brecher" title="Irving Brecher">Irving Brecher</a>, <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Fields" title="Herbert Fields">Herbert Fields</a>, Arthur Freed, Yip Harburg, <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Hoffenstein" title="Samuel Hoffenstein">Samuel Hoffenstein</a>, Jack Mintz, Sid Silvers, <a href="/wiki/Richard_Thorpe" title="Richard Thorpe">Richard Thorpe</a>, Cukor and Vidor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic_13-2"><a href="#cite_note-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic-13">[13]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, songwriter Harburg's son (and biographer) Ernie Harburg reported:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-democracynow.org_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-democracynow.org-17">[17]</a></sup></p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>So anyhow, Yip also wrote all the dialogue in that time and the setup to the songs and he also wrote the part where they give out the heart, the brains, and the nerve, because he was the final script editor. And he – there was eleven screenwriters on that – and he pulled the whole thing together, wrote his own lines and gave the thing a coherence and unity which made it a work of art. But he doesn't get credit for that. He gets lyrics by E. Y. Harburg, you see. But nevertheless, he put his influence on the thing.</p>
</blockquote>
<p>The original producers thought that a 1939 audience was too sophisticated to accept Oz as a straight-ahead fantasy; therefore, it was reconceived as a lengthy, elaborate <a href="/wiki/Dream_sequence" title="Dream sequence">dream sequence</a>. Because of a perceived need to attract a youthful audience through appealing to modern fads and styles, the score had featured a song called "<a href="/wiki/The_Jitterbug" title="The Jitterbug">The Jitterbug</a>", and the script had featured a scene with a series of musical contests. A spoiled, selfish princess in Oz had outlawed all forms of music except classical and <a href="/wiki/Operetta" title="Operetta">operetta</a>, and went up against Dorothy in a singing contest in which her swing style enchanted listeners and won the grand prize. This part was initially written for <a href="/wiki/Betty_Jaynes_(actress)" title="Betty Jaynes (actress)">Betty Jaynes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup> The plan was later dropped.</p>
<p>Another scene, which was removed before final script approval and never filmed, was a concluding scene back in Kansas after Dorothy's return. Hunk (the Kansan counterpart to the Scarecrow) is leaving for agricultural college and extracts a promise from Dorothy to write to him. The implication of the scene is that romance will eventually develop between the two, which also may have been intended as an explanation for Dorothy's partiality for the Scarecrow over her other two companions. This plot idea was never totally dropped, but is especially noticeable in the final script when Dorothy, just before she is to leave Oz, tells the Scarecrow, "I think I'll miss you most of all."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>
<p>In his book <i>The Wonderful Wizard of Oz</i>, Baum describes Kansas as being "in shades of gray". Further, Dorothy lived inside a farmhouse which had its paint blistered and washed away by the weather, giving it an air of grayness. The house and property were situated in the middle of a sweeping prairie where the grass was burnt gray by harsh sun. Aunt Em and Uncle Henry were "gray with age". Effectively, the use of monochrome sepia tones for the Kansas sequences was a stylistic choice that evoked the dull and gray countryside.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Much attention was given to the use of color in the production, with the MGM production crew favoring some hues over others. Consequently, it took the studio's art department almost a week to settle on the final shade of yellow used for the yellow brick road.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Casting">Casting</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Casting">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>LeRoy had always insisted that he wanted to cast <a href="/wiki/Judy_Garland" title="Judy Garland">Judy Garland</a> to play Dorothy from the start; however, evidence suggests that negotiations occurred early in pre-production for <a href="/wiki/Shirley_Temple" title="Shirley Temple">Shirley Temple</a> to be cast as Dorothy, on loan from <a href="/wiki/20th_Century_Fox" title="20th Century Fox">20th Century Fox</a>. A persistent rumor also existed that Fox, in turn, was promised <a href="/wiki/Clark_Gable" title="Clark Gable">Clark Gable</a> and <a href="/wiki/Jean_Harlow" title="Jean Harlow">Jean Harlow</a> as a loan from MGM. The tale is almost certainly untrue, as Harlow died in 1937, before MGM had even purchased the rights to the story. Despite this, the story appears in many film biographies (including Temple's own autobiography). The documentary <i>The Wonderful Wizard of Oz: The Making of a Movie Classic</i> states that Mervyn LeRoy was under pressure to cast Temple, then the most popular child star, but at an unofficial audition, MGM musical mainstay <a href="/wiki/Roger_Edens" title="Roger Edens">Roger Edens</a> listened to her sing and felt that an actress with a different style was needed. Newsreel footage is included in which Temple wisecracks, "There's no place like home", suggesting that she was being considered for the part at that time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> A possibility is that this consideration did indeed take place, but that Gable and Harlow were not part of the proposed deal.</p>
<p>Actress <a href="/wiki/Deanna_Durbin" title="Deanna Durbin">Deanna Durbin</a>, who was under contract to <a href="/wiki/Universal_Pictures" title="Universal Pictures">Universal Studios</a>, was also considered for the part of Dorothy. Durbin, at the time, far exceeded Garland in film experience and fan base and both had co-starred in a 1936 <a href="/wiki/Short_film" title="Short film">two-reeler</a> titled <i><a href="/wiki/Every_Sunday" title="Every Sunday">Every Sunday</a></i>. The film was most notable for exhibiting Durbin's operatic style of singing against Garland's jazzier style. Durbin was possibly passed over once it was decided to bring on Jaynes, also an operatic singer, to rival Garland's jazz in the aforementioned discarded subplot of the film.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="392" data-file-width="300" height="222" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg/170px-Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg/255px-Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/7/71/Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddy_Ebsen_Tin_Man.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Buddy_Ebsen" title="Buddy Ebsen">Buddy Ebsen</a>'s first makeup test as the Tin Man</div>
</div>
</div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Ray_Bolger" title="Ray Bolger">Ray Bolger</a> was originally cast as the Tin Man and <a href="/wiki/Buddy_Ebsen" title="Buddy Ebsen">Buddy Ebsen</a> was to play the Scarecrow.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timeline_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-timeline-16">[16]</a></sup> Bolger, however, longed to play the Scarecrow, as his childhood idol <a href="/wiki/Fred_Stone" title="Fred Stone">Fred Stone</a> had done <a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1902_musical)" title="The Wizard of Oz (1902 musical)">on stage in 1902</a>; with that very performance, Stone had inspired him to become a vaudevillian in the first place. Now unhappy with his role as the Tin Man (reportedly claiming, "I'm not a tin performer; I'm fluid"), Bolger convinced producer Mervyn LeRoy to recast him in the part he so desired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cemeteryguide.com_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-cemeteryguide.com-22">[22]</a></sup> Ebsen did not object; after going over the basics of the Scarecrow's distinctive gait with Bolger (as a professional dancer, Ebsen had been cast because the studio was confident he would be up to the task of replicating the famous "wobbly-walk" of Stone's Scarecrow), he recorded all of his songs, went through all the rehearsals as the Tin Man and began filming with the rest of the cast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Bert_Lahr" title="Bert Lahr">Bert Lahr</a> was signed for the Cowardly Lion on July 25, 1938; the next month, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Charles_Grapewin" title="Charles Grapewin">Charles Grapewin</a> was cast as Uncle Henry on August 12.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/W._C._Fields" title="W. C. Fields">W. C. Fields</a> was originally chosen for the role of the Wizard, a role turned down by <a href="/wiki/Ed_Wynn" title="Ed Wynn">Ed Wynn</a> as he thought the part was too small, but the studio ran out of patience after protracted haggling over Fields' fee; instead, another contract player, <a href="/wiki/Frank_Morgan" title="Frank Morgan">Frank Morgan</a>, was cast on September 22.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Gale_Sondergaard" title="Gale Sondergaard">Gale Sondergaard</a> was originally cast as the Wicked Witch. She became unhappy when the witch's persona shifted from sly and glamorous (thought to emulate the wicked queen in Disney's <i>Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</i>) into the familiar "ugly hag". She turned down the role and was replaced on October 10, 1938, just three days before filming started, by MGM contract player <a href="/wiki/Margaret_Hamilton_(actress)" title="Margaret Hamilton (actress)">Margaret Hamilton</a>. Sondergaard said in an interview for a bonus feature on the <a href="/wiki/DVD" title="DVD">DVD</a> that she had no regrets about turning down the part, and would go on to play a glamorous villain in Fox's version of <a href="/wiki/Maurice_Maeterlinck" title="Maurice Maeterlinck">Maurice Maeterlinck</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Blue_Bird_(1940_film)" title="The Blue Bird (1940 film)">The Blue Bird</a></i> in 1940; Margaret Hamilton played a role remarkably similar to the Wicked Witch in the Judy Garland film <i><a href="/wiki/Babes_in_Arms_(film)" title="Babes in Arms (film)">Babes in Arms</a></i> (1939).</p>
<p>According to Aljean Harmetz, the "gone-to-seed" coat worn by Morgan as the wizard was selected from a rack of coats purchased from a second-hand shop. According to legend, Morgan later discovered a label in the coat indicating it had once belonged to Baum, that Baum's widow confirmed this, and that the coat was eventually presented to her. But Baum biographer <a href="/wiki/Michael_Patrick_Hearn" title="Michael Patrick Hearn">Michael Patrick Hearn</a> says the Baum family denies ever seeing the coat or knowing of the story; Hamilton considered it a concocted studio rumor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Filming">Filming</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Filming">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Richard_Thorpe_as_director">Richard Thorpe as director</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Richard Thorpe as director">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Filming commenced October 13, 1938, on the MGM lot in <a href="/wiki/Culver_City,_California" title="Culver City, California">Culver City, California</a>, under the direction of <a href="/wiki/Richard_Thorpe" title="Richard Thorpe">Richard Thorpe</a> (replacing original director <a href="/wiki/Norman_Taurog" title="Norman Taurog">Norman Taurog</a>, who filmed only a few early Technicolor tests and was then reassigned). Thorpe initially shot about two weeks of footage (nine days in total) involving Dorothy's first encounter with the Scarecrow, as well as a number of sequences in the Wicked Witch's castle, such as Dorothy's rescue (which, though unreleased, comprises the only footage of Ebsen's Tin Man).</p>
<p>According to most sources, ten days into the shoot, Ebsen suffered a reaction to the aluminum powder makeup he wore. He was hospitalized in critical condition, and subsequently was forced to leave the project; in a later interview (included on the 2005 DVD release of <i>The Wizard of Oz</i>), he recalled the studio heads appreciated the seriousness of his illness only after seeing him in the hospital. Filming halted while a replacement for him was found. No full footage of him as the Tin Man has ever been released – only photographs taken during filming and makeup test photos. His replacement, <a href="/wiki/Jack_Haley" title="Jack Haley">Jack Haley</a>, simply assumed he had been fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup> Author and screen-writer <a href="/wiki/George_MacDonald_Fraser" title="George MacDonald Fraser">George MacDonald Fraser</a> offers an alternative story, told to him by <a href="/wiki/Burt_Lancaster" title="Burt Lancaster">Burt Lancaster</a>'s producing partner, Jim Hill, that Ebsen had refused to be painted silver and was fired.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="George_Cukor's_brief_stint"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="George_Cukor.27s_brief_stint">George Cukor's brief stint</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: George Cukor's brief stint">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>LeRoy, after reviewing the footage and feeling Thorpe was rushing the production, adversely affecting the actors' performances, had Thorpe replaced. During reorganization on the production, <a href="/wiki/George_Cukor" title="George Cukor">George Cukor</a> temporarily took over, under LeRoy's guidance. Initially, the studio had made Garland wear a blond wig and heavy "baby-doll" makeup, and she played Dorothy in an exaggerated fashion; now, Cukor changed Garland's and Hamilton's makeup and costumes, and told Garland to "be herself". This meant that all the scenes Garland and Hamilton had already completed had to be discarded and reshot. Cukor also suggested that the studio cast Jack Haley, on loan from Fox, as the Tin Man. To keep down on production costs, Haley only rerecorded "If I Only Had a Heart" and solo lines during "The Jitterbug" and "If I Only Had the Nerve"; as such, Ebsen's voice can still be heard in the remaining songs featuring the Tin Man in group vocals. The makeup used for Haley was quietly changed to an aluminum paste, with a layer of clown white greasepaint underneath to protect his skin; although it did not have the same dire effect on Haley, he did at one point suffer an eye infection from it.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2014)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In addition, Bolger's original recording of "<a href="/wiki/If_I_Only_Had_a_Brain" title="If I Only Had a Brain">If I Only Had a Brain</a>" had been far more sedate compared to the version heard in the film; during this time, Cukor and LeRoy decided that a more energetic rendition would better suit Dorothy's initial meeting with the Scarecrow (initially, it was to contrast with his lively manner in Thorpe's footage), and was rerecorded as such. At first thought to be lost for over seven decades, a recording of this original version was rediscovered in 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup></p>
<h4><span id="Victor_Fleming,_the_main_director"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Victor_Fleming.2C_the_main_director">Victor Fleming, the main director</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Victor Fleming, the main director">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="279" data-file-width="357" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg/170px-Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg/255px-Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/7/71/Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg/340px-Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_of_Oz_01.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Director Victor Fleming on set with cast</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Cukor did not actually shoot any scenes for the film, merely acting as something of a "creative advisor" to the troubled production, and, because of his prior commitment to direct <i>Gone with the Wind</i>, he left on November 3, 1938, when <a href="/wiki/Victor_Fleming" title="Victor Fleming">Victor Fleming</a> assumed directorial responsibility. As director, Fleming chose not to shift the film from Cukor's creative realignment, as producer LeRoy had already pronounced his satisfaction with the new course the film was taking.</p>
<p>Production on the bulk of the Technicolor sequences was a long and cumbersome process that ran for over six months, from October 1938 to March 1939. Most of the cast worked six days a week and had to arrive as early as 4:00 a.m. to be fitted with makeup and costumes, and often did not leave until 7 pm or later. Cumbersome makeup and costumes were made even more uncomfortable by the daylight-bright lighting the early Technicolor process required, which could heat the set to over 100 °F (38 °C). Bolger later said that the frightening nature of the costumes prevented most of the Oz principals from eating in the studio commissary;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> the toxicity of Hamilton's copper-based makeup forced her to eat a liquid diet on shoot days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> It took as many as twelve takes to have Toto run alongside the actors as they skipped down the yellow brick road.</p>
<p>All of the Oz sequences were filmed in three-strip Technicolor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic_13-3"><a href="#cite_note-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harmetz_14-3"><a href="#cite_note-Harmetz-14">[14]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Opening_credits" title="Opening credits">opening</a> and <a href="/wiki/Closing_credits" title="Closing credits">closing credits</a>, as well as the Kansas sequences, were filmed in black and white and colored in a sepia-tone process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic_13-4"><a href="#cite_note-TheMakingOfAMovieClassic-13">[13]</a></sup> Sepia-toned film was also used in the scene where Aunt Em appears in the Wicked Witch's crystal ball.</p>
<p>An extensive talent search produced over a hundred little people to play Munchkins; this meant that most of the film's Oz sequences would have to already be shot before work on the Munchkinland sequence could begin. According to Munchkin actor <a href="/wiki/Jerry_Maren" title="Jerry Maren">Jerry Maren</a>, the little people were each paid over $125 a week. <a href="/wiki/Meinhardt_Raabe" title="Meinhardt Raabe">Meinhardt Raabe</a>, who played the coroner, revealed in the 1990 documentary <i>The Making of the Wizard of Oz</i> that the MGM costume and wardrobe department, under the direction of designer <a href="/wiki/Adrian_(costume_designer)" title="Adrian (costume designer)">Adrian</a>, had to design over 100 costumes for the Munchkin sequences. They then had to photograph and catalog each Munchkin in his or her costume so that they could correctly apply the same costume and makeup each day of production.</p>
<p>In Hamilton's exit from the Munchkinland a concealed elevator was arranged to lower her below stage as fire and smoke erupted to dramatize and conceal her exit. The first take ran well but in the second take the timing of the elevator was off; the flames set fire to her greasy makeup, causing second-degree burns on her hands and face. She spent six weeks in the hospital.</p>
<h4><span id="King_Vidor's_finishing_work_as_director"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="King_Vidor.27s_finishing_work_as_director">King Vidor's finishing work as director</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: King Vidor's finishing work as director">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>On February 12, 1939, Fleming hastily replaced Cukor in directing <i>Gone with the Wind</i>; the next day, <a href="/wiki/King_Vidor" title="King Vidor">King Vidor</a> was assigned as director by the studio to finish the filming of <i>The Wizard of Oz</i> (mainly the sepia-toned Kansas sequences, including Garland's singing of "<a href="/wiki/Over_the_Rainbow" title="Over the Rainbow">Over the Rainbow</a>" and the tornado). In later years, when the film became firmly established as a classic, Vidor chose not to take public credit for his contribution until after the death of his friend Fleming in 1949.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-production">Post-production</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Post-production">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Principal_photography" title="Principal photography">Principal photography</a> concluded with the Kansas sequences on March 16, 1939; nonetheless, reshoots and pick-up shots were filmed throughout April and May and into June, under the direction of producer LeRoy. After the deletion of the "Over the Rainbow" reprise during subsequent test screenings in early June, Garland had to be brought back one more time to reshoot the "Auntie Em, I'm frightened!" scene without the song; the footage of Blandick's Aunt Em, as shot by Vidor, had already been set aside for rear-projection work, and was simply reused.</p>
<p>After Hamilton's torturous experience with the Munchkinland elevator, she refused to do the pick-ups for the scene in which she flies on a broomstick that billows smoke, so LeRoy chose to have stand-in Betty Danko perform the scene, instead; as a result, Danko was severely injured doing the scene due to a malfunction in the smoke mechanism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup></p>
<p>At this point, the film began a long arduous post-production. <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Stothart" title="Herbert Stothart">Herbert Stothart</a> had to compose the film's background score, while <a href="/wiki/A._Arnold_Gillespie" title="A. Arnold Gillespie">A. Arnold Gillespie</a> had to perfect the various special effects that the film required, including many of the rear projection shots. The MGM art department also had to create the various matte paintings for the background of many of the scenes.</p>
<p>One significant innovation planned for the film was the use of stencil printing for the transition to Technicolor. Each frame was to be hand-tinted to maintain the sepia tone; however, because this was too expensive and labor-intensive, it was abandoned and MGM used a simpler and less expensive variation of the process. During the reshoots in May, the inside of the farm house was painted sepia, and when Dorothy opens the door, it is not Garland, but her stand-in, Bobbie Koshay, wearing a sepia gingham dress, who then backs out of frame; once the camera moves through the door, Garland steps back into frame in her bright blue gingham dress (as noted in DVD extras), and the sepia-painted door briefly tints her with the same color before she emerges from the house's shadow, into the bright glare of the Technicolor lighting. This also meant that the reshoots provided the first proper shot of Munchkinland; if one looks carefully, the brief cut to Dorothy looking around outside the house bisects a single long shot, from the inside of the doorway to the pan-around that finally ends in a reverse-angle as the ruins of the house are seen behind Dorothy as she comes to a stop at the foot of the small bridge.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Test_screening" title="Test screening">Test screenings</a> of the film began on June 5, 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-geo_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-geo-31">[31]</a></sup> <i>Oz</i> initially ran nearly two hours long. LeRoy and Fleming knew that at least a quarter of an hour needed to be deleted to get the film down to a manageable running time; the average film in 1939 running just about 90 minutes. Three sneak previews in <a href="/wiki/Santa_Barbara,_California" title="Santa Barbara, California">Santa Barbara</a>, <a href="/wiki/Pomona,_California" title="Pomona, California">Pomona</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Luis_Obispo,_California" title="San Luis Obispo, California">San Luis Obispo, California</a>, helped guide LeRoy and Fleming in the cutting. Among the many cuts were "The Jitterbug" number, the Scarecrow's elaborate dance sequence following "If I Only Had a Brain", reprises of "Over the Rainbow" and "<a href="/wiki/Ding-Dong!_The_Witch_Is_Dead" title="Ding-Dong! The Witch Is Dead">Ding-Dong! The Witch Is Dead</a>", and a number of smaller dialogue sequences. This left the final, mostly serious portion of the film with no songs, only the dramatic underscoring.</p>
<p>One song that was almost deleted was "Over the Rainbow". MGM had felt that it made the Kansas sequence too long, as well as being far over the heads of the target audience of children. The studio also thought that it was degrading for Garland to sing in a barnyard. LeRoy, uncredited associate producer Arthur Freed and director Fleming fought to keep it in, and they all eventually won. The song went on to win the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academy_Award" title="Academy Award">Academy Award</a> for Best Song of the Year, and came to be identified so strongly with Garland herself that she made it her theme song. In 2004, the song was ranked no. 1 by the American Film Institute on <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Songs" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Songs">AFI's 100 Years...100 Songs</a> list.</p>
<p>After the preview in San Luis Obispo in early July, the film was officially released in August 1939 at its current 101-minute running time.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Music">Music</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Music">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Musical_selections_in_The_Wizard_of_Oz" title="Musical selections in The Wizard of Oz">Musical selections in The Wizard of Oz</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vc41.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="510" data-file-width="640" height="207" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Vc41.jpg/260px-Vc41.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Vc41.jpg/390px-Vc41.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Vc41.jpg/520px-Vc41.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vc41.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Herbert Stothart conducts the MGM Studio Orchestra for <i>The Wizard of Oz</i>, which was recorded at the MGM studios.</div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1069" data-file-width="1373" height="202" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg/260px-Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg/390px-Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg/520px-Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg 2x" width="260"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_of_Oz_lobby_card.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Lobby card with still of deleted musical number "Hail! Hail! The Witch is Dead!", sung upon the triumphant return to Emerald City</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The film is widely noted for its musical selections and soundtrack. The music was composed by <a href="/wiki/Harold_Arlen" title="Harold Arlen">Harold Arlen</a>, and the lyrics were written by <a href="/wiki/Yip_Harburg" title="Yip Harburg">Yip Harburg</a>, both of whom won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Song" title="Academy Award for Best Original Song">Academy Award for Best Original Song</a> for "Over the Rainbow". The song was ranked first in two lists: the <a href="/wiki/AFI%27s_100_Years...100_Songs" title="AFI's 100 Years...100 Songs">AFI's 100 Years...100 Songs</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Recording_Industry_Association_of_America" title="Recording Industry Association of America">Recording Industry Association of America</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Songs_of_the_Century" title="Songs of the Century">365 Songs of the Century</a>".</p>
<p>MGM composer <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Stothart" title="Herbert Stothart">Herbert Stothart</a>, a well-known Hollywood composer and songwriter, won the <a href="/wiki/Academy_Award_for_Best_Original_Score" title="Academy Award for Best Original Score">Academy Award for Best Original Score</a> in recognition of his original score.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Georgie_Stoll" title="Georgie Stoll">Georgie Stoll</a> was associate conductor and screen credit was given to <a href="/wiki/George_Bassman" title="George Bassman">George Bassman</a>, <a href="/wiki/Murray_Cutter" title="Murray Cutter">Murray Cutter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ken_Darby" title="Ken Darby">Ken Darby</a> and Paul Marquardt for orchestral and vocal arrangements (as usual, Roger Edens was also heavily involved as an unbilled musical associate to Freed.)</p>
<p>The song "The Jitterbug", written in a swing style, was intended for the sequence in which the group is journeying to the Witch's castle. Due to time constraints, the song was cut from the final theatrical version. The film footage for the song has been lost, although silent home film footage of rehearsals for the number has survived. The sound recording for the song, however, is intact and was included in the two-CD <a href="/wiki/Rhino_Entertainment" title="Rhino Entertainment">Rhino Records</a> deluxe edition of the film soundtrack, as well as on the VHS and DVD editions of the film. A reference to "The Jitterbug" remains in the film: the Witch remarks to her flying monkeys that they should have no trouble apprehending Dorothy and her friends because "I've sent a little insect on ahead to take the fight out of them."</p>
<p>Another musical number cut before release occurred right after the Wicked Witch of the West was melted and before Dorothy and her friends returned to the Wizard. This was a reprise of "Ding-Dong! The Witch Is Dead" (blended with "<a href="/wiki/We%27re_Off_to_See_the_Wizard" title="We're Off to See the Wizard">We're Off to See the Wizard</a>" and "The Merry Old Land of Oz") with the lyrics altered to "Hail! Hail! The Witch is Dead!" This started with the Witch's guard saying "Hail to Dorothy! The Wicked Witch is dead!" and dissolved to a huge celebration of the citizens of the Emerald City singing the song as they accompany Dorothy and her friends to see the Wizard. Today, the film of this scene is also lost and only a few stills survive, along with a few seconds of footage used on several reissue trailers. The entire audio still exists and is included on the two-CD Rhino Record deluxe edition of the film soundtrack.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, a brief reprise of "Over the Rainbow" was intended to be sung by Garland while Dorothy is trapped in the Witch's castle, but it was cut because it was considered too emotionally intense. The original soundtrack recording still exists, however, and was included as an extra in all home media releases from 1993-onwards.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup></p>
<p>The songs were recorded in the studio's scoring stage before filming. Several of the recordings were completed while Ebsen was still with the cast. Therefore, while he had to be dropped from the cast due to illness from the aluminum powder makeup, his singing voice remained in the soundtrack (as noted in the notes for the CD Deluxe Edition). In the group vocals of "We're Off to See the Wizard", his voice can be heard. Haley spoke with a distinct Boston accent, thus did not pronounce the <i>r</i> in <i>wizard</i>. By contrast, Ebsen was a <a href="/wiki/Midwestern_United_States" title="Midwestern United States">Midwesterner</a>, like Garland, and pronounced it. Haley rerecorded Ebsen's solo parts later.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="773" data-file-width="966" height="136" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg/170px-Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg/255px-Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg/340px-Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Judy_Garland_Over_the_Rainbow_2.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Judy Garland singing "Over the Rainbow" with Terry</div>
</div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Song_list">Song list</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Song list">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<ul>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Over_the_Rainbow" title="Over the Rainbow">Over the Rainbow</a>" – Judy Garland as Dorothy Gale</li>
<li>Munchkinland Sequence:
<ul>
<li>"Come Out ..." – Billie Burke as Glinda, and the Munchkins</li>
<li>"It Really Was No Miracle" – Judy Garland as Dorothy, Billy Bletcher and the Munchkins</li>
<li>"We Thank You Very Sweetly" – Frank Cucksey and Joseph Koziel</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Ding-Dong!_The_Witch_Is_Dead" title="Ding-Dong! The Witch Is Dead">Ding-Dong! The Witch Is Dead</a>" – Billie Burke as Glinda (speaking) and the Munchkins</li>
<li>"As Mayor of the Munchkin City"</li>
<li>"As Coroner, I Must Aver"</li>
<li>"Ding-Dong! The Witch Is Dead" (Reprise) – The Munchkins</li>
<li>"The Lullaby League"</li>
<li>"The Lollipop Guild"</li>
<li>"We Welcome You to Munchkinland" – The Munchkins</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li>"Follow the Yellow Brick Road/You're Off to See the Wizard" – Judy Garland as Dorothy, and the Munchkins</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/If_I_Only_Had_a_Brain" title="If I Only Had a Brain">If I Only Had a Brain</a>" – Ray Bolger as the Scarecrow, and Judy Garland as Dorothy</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/We%27re_Off_to_See_the_Wizard" title="We're Off to See the Wizard">We're Off to See the Wizard</a>" – Judy Garland as Dorothy, and Ray Bolger as the Scarecrow</li>
<li>"If I Only Had a Heart" – Jack Haley as the Tin Man</li>
<li>"If I Only Had a Heart" (<i>original recording</i>) – Buddy Ebsen as the Tin Man</li>
<li>"We're Off to See the Wizard" (Reprise 1) – Judy Garland as Dorothy, Ray Bolger as the Scarecrow, and Buddy Ebsen as the Tin Man</li>
<li>"If I Only Had the Nerve" – Bert Lahr as the Cowardly Lion, Jack Haley as the Tin Man, Ray Bolger as the Scarecrow, and Judy Garland as Dorothy</li>
<li>"We're Off to See the Wizard" (Reprise 2) – Judy Garland as Dorothy, Ray Bolger as the Scarecrow, Buddy Ebsen as the Tin Man, and Bert Lahr as the Cowardly Lion</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Optimistic_Voices" title="Optimistic Voices">Optimistic Voices</a>" – MGM Studio Chorus</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/The_Merry_Old_Land_of_Oz" title="The Merry Old Land of Oz">The Merry Old Land of Oz</a>" – Frank Morgan as Cabby, Judy Garland as Dorothy, Ray Bolger as Scarecrow, Jack Haley as the Tin Man, Bert Lahr as the Cowardly Lion and the Emerald City townspeople</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/If_I_Were_King_of_the_Forest" title="If I Were King of the Forest">If I Were King of the Forest</a>" – Bert Lahr as the Cowardly Lion, Judy Garland as Dorothy, Ray Bolger as the Scarecrow and Jack Haley as the Tin Man</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/The_Jitterbug" title="The Jitterbug">The Jitterbug</a>" – Although this song was removed from the final film, it is still available on some extended edition CDs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Underscoring">Underscoring</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Underscoring">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Extensive edits in the film's final cut removed vocals from the last portion of the film. However, the film was fully <a href="/wiki/Underscoring" title="Underscoring">underscored</a>, with instrumental snippets from the film's various <a href="/wiki/Leitmotif" title="Leitmotif">leitmotifs</a> throughout. There was also some recognizable popular music, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Excerpts from <a href="/wiki/Robert_Schumann" title="Robert Schumann">Schumann</a>'s "The Happy Farmer", at several points early in the film, including the opening scene when Dorothy and Toto are hurrying home after their encounter with Miss Gulch, when Toto escapes from her, and when the house is "riding" the tornado.</li>
<li>An excerpt of <a href="/wiki/Felix_Mendelssohn" title="Felix Mendelssohn">Mendelssohn</a>'s "Opus 16, #2", when Toto escapes from the Witch's castle.</li>
<li>An excerpt of <a href="/wiki/Modest_Mussorgsky" title="Modest Mussorgsky">Mussorgsky</a>'s "<a href="/wiki/Night_on_Bald_Mountain" title="Night on Bald Mountain">Night on Bald Mountain</a>", when Dorothy, the Scarecrow, the Tin Man, and the Cowardly Lion are trying to escape from the Witch's castle.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/In_the_Shade_of_the_Old_Apple_Tree" title="In the Shade of the Old Apple Tree">In the Shade of the Old Apple Tree</a>", when Dorothy and the Scarecrow discover the anthropomorphic apple trees.</li>
<li>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaudeamus_Igitur" title="Gaudeamus Igitur">Gaudeamus Igitur</a>" during the Wizard's presentation of awards to the group.</li>
<li>"<a href="/wiki/Home!_Sweet_Home!" title="Home! Sweet Home!">Home! Sweet Home!</a>", in part of the underscore of the closing scene, at Dorothy's house in Kansas.</li>
</ul>
<p>(The above list is excerpted from the liner notes on the Rhino Records collection.)</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Release">Release</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Release">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_of_Oz_(Oconomowoc)_cropped.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="1433" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wizard_of_Oz_%28Oconomowoc%29_cropped.jpg/170px-Wizard_of_Oz_%28Oconomowoc%29_cropped.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wizard_of_Oz_%28Oconomowoc%29_cropped.jpg/255px-Wizard_of_Oz_%28Oconomowoc%29_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/18/Wizard_of_Oz_%28Oconomowoc%29_cropped.jpg/340px-Wizard_of_Oz_%28Oconomowoc%29_cropped.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_of_Oz_(Oconomowoc)_cropped.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
A memorial commemorating the film's world premiere at the Strand Theatre in <a href="/wiki/Oconomowoc,_Wisconsin" title="Oconomowoc, Wisconsin">Oconomowoc, Wisconsin</a> on August 12, 1939</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The film's first sneak preview was held in San Bernardino, California.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup> The film was previewed in three <a href="/wiki/Test_market" title="Test market">test markets</a>: on August 11, 1939, at <a href="/wiki/Kenosha,_Wisconsin" title="Kenosha, Wisconsin">Kenosha, Wisconsin</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cape_Cod,_Massachusetts" title="Cape Cod, Massachusetts">Cape Cod, Massachusetts</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mad2009_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-mad2009-37">[37]</a></sup> and at the Strand Theatre in <a href="/wiki/Oconomowoc,_Wisconsin" title="Oconomowoc, Wisconsin">Oconomowoc, Wisconsin</a>, on August 12.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-wsj_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-wsj-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>The Hollywood premiere was on August 15, 1939,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mad2009_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-mad2009-37">[37]</a></sup> at <a href="/wiki/Grauman%27s_Chinese_Theatre" title="Grauman's Chinese Theatre">Grauman's Chinese Theatre</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> The New York City premiere, held at <a href="/wiki/Capitol_Theatre_(New_York_City)" title="Capitol Theatre (New York City)">Loew's Capitol Theatre</a> on August 17, 1939, was followed by a live performance with Garland and her frequent film co-star <a href="/wiki/Mickey_Rooney" title="Mickey Rooney">Mickey Rooney</a>. They continued to perform there after each screening for a week, extended in Rooney's case for a second week and in Garland's to three (with <i>Oz</i> co-stars Ray Bolger and Bert Lahr replacing Rooney for the third and final week). The film opened nationwide on August 25, 1939.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Box_office">Box office</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Box office">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>According to MGM records, during the film's initial release, it earned $2,048,000 in the US and Canada and $969,000 in other countries throughout the world, resulting total earnings of $3,017,000. While these were considerable earnings, the high production cost, in association with various distribution and other costs, meant the movie initially recorded a loss of $1,145,000 for the studio.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mannix_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-Mannix-3">[3]</a></sup> It did not show what MGM considered a profit until a 1949 rerelease earned an additional $1.5 million (about $15 million today). However, for all the risks and cost that MGM undertook to produce the film, it was considered at least more successful than anyone thought it would be. According to Christopher Finch, author of the Judy Garland biography <i>Rainbow: The Stormy Life Of Judy Garland</i>, "Fantasy is always a risk at the box office. The film had been enormously successful as a book, and it had also been a major stage hit, but previous attempts to bring it the screen had been dismal failures." Finch also writes that after the success of the film, Garland signed a new contract with MGM giving her a substantial increase in salary, making her one of the top-ten box office stars in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception">Reception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The film received much acclaim upon its release. <a href="/wiki/Frank_Nugent" title="Frank Nugent">Frank Nugent</a> considered the film a "delightful piece of wonder-working which had the youngsters' eyes shining and brought a quietly amused gleam to the wiser ones of the oldsters. Not since Disney's <i>Snow White and the Seven Dwarfs</i> has anything quite so fantastic succeeded half so well."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt1939_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1939-41">[41]</a></sup> Nugent had issues with some of the film's special effects, writing, "with the best of will and ingenuity, they cannot make a Munchkin or a Flying Monkey that will not still suggest, however vaguely, a Singer's Midget in a <a href="/wiki/Jack_Dawn" title="Jack Dawn">Jack Dawn</a> masquerade. Nor can they, without a few betraying jolts and split-screen overlappings, bring down from the sky the great soap bubble in which Glinda rides and roll it smoothly into place." According to Nugent, "Judy Garland's Dorothy is a pert and fresh-faced miss with the wonder-lit eyes of a believer in fairy tales, but the Baum fantasy is at its best when the Scarecrow, the Tin Man, and the Lion are on the move."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-nyt1939_41-1"><a href="#cite_note-nyt1939-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writing in <i><a href="/wiki/Variety_(magazine)" title="Variety (magazine)">Variety</a></i>, John C. Flinn predicted that the film was "likely to perform some record-breaking feats of box-office magic," noting, "Some of the scenic passages are so beautiful in design and composition as to stir audiences by their sheer unfoldment." He also called Garland "an appealing figure" and the musical numbers "gay and bright."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p><i><a href="/wiki/Harrison%27s_Reports" title="Harrison's Reports">Harrison's Reports</a></i> wrote, "Even though some persons are not interested in pictures of this type, it is possible that they will be eager to see this picture just for its technical treatment. The performances are good, and the incidental music is of considerable aid. Pictures of this caliber bring credit to the industry."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup></p>
<p>"<a href="/wiki/Leo_the_Lion_(MGM)" title="Leo the Lion (MGM)">Leo the Lion</a> is privileged to herald this one with his deepest roar - the one that comes from way down - for seldom if indeed ever has the screen been so successful in its approach to fantasy and extravaganza through flesh-and-blood," wrote <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_Daily" title="Film Daily">Film Daily</a></i>, adding that this "handsomely mounted fairy story in Technicolor, with its wealth of humor and homespun philosophy, its stimulus to the imagination, its procession of unforgettable settings, its studding of merry tunes should click solidly at the box-office."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>
<p>Not all reviews were positive. Some moviegoers felt that the 16-year-old Garland was slightly too old to play the little girl who Baum originally intended his Dorothy to be. Russell Maloney of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Yorker" title="The New Yorker">The New Yorker</a></i> wrote that the film displayed "no trace of imagination, good taste, or ingenuity" and declared it "a stinkeroo,"<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> while Otis Ferguson of <i><a href="/wiki/The_New_Republic" title="The New Republic">The New Republic</a></i> wrote, "It has dwarfs, music, Technicolor, freak characters, and Judy Garland. It can't be expected to have a sense of humor, as well - and as for the light touch of fantasy, it weighs like a pound of fruitcake soaking wet."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> The film placed seventh on <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Film_Daily" title="Film Daily">Film Daily</a>'</i>s year-end nationwide poll of 542 critics naming the best films of 1939.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Roger_Ebert" title="Roger Ebert">Roger Ebert</a> chose it as one of his Great Films, writing that "<i>The Wizard of Oz</i> has a wonderful surface of comedy and music, special effects and excitement, but we still watch it six decades later because its underlying story penetrates straight to the deepest insecurities of childhood, stirs them and then reassures them."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ebert_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ebert-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<p>Writer <a href="/wiki/Salman_Rushdie" title="Salman Rushdie">Salman Rushdie</a> acknowledged "<i>The Wizard of Oz</i> was my very first literary influence" in his 2002 musings about the film.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> He has written: "When I first saw <i>The Wizard of Oz</i>, it made a writer of me."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rushdie_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rushdie-50">[50]</a></sup> His first short story, written at the age of 10, was titled "Over the Rainbow".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rushdie_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Rushdie-50">[50]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a 2009 retrospective article about the film, <i><a href="/wiki/San_Francisco_Chronicle" title="San Francisco Chronicle">San Francisco Chronicle</a></i> film critic and author <a href="/wiki/Mick_LaSalle" title="Mick LaSalle">Mick LaSalle</a> declared that the film's "entire <a href="/wiki/Munchkin_Country" title="Munchkin Country">Munchkinland</a> sequence, from Dorothy's arrival in Oz to her departure on the yellow brick road, has to be one of the greatest in cinema history – a masterpiece of set design, costuming, choreography, music, lyrics, storytelling, and sheer imagination."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup></p>
<p>On the film critic aggregator site <a href="/wiki/Rotten_Tomatoes" title="Rotten Tomatoes">Rotten Tomatoes</a>, the film has an approval rating of 99% based on 109 reviews, with an average score of 9.4/10. The site's critical consensus reads, "An absolute masterpiece whose groundbreaking visuals and deft storytelling are still every bit as resonant, <i>The Wizard of Oz</i> is a must-see film for young and old."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> At <a href="/wiki/Metacritic" title="Metacritic">Metacritic</a>, which assigns a normalized rating to reviews, the film received the maximum score of 100 out of 100, based on 4 reviews, indicating "universal acclaim",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> which, as of August 2017, is matched only by five other films.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_title_page.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="400" data-file-width="288" height="236" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Wizard_title_page.jpg/170px-Wizard_title_page.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d2/Wizard_title_page.jpg/255px-Wizard_title_page.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d2/Wizard_title_page.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wizard_title_page.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The Wonderful Wizard of Oz, by L. Frank Baum</div>
</div>
</div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Re-releases">Re-releases</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Re-releases">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Although the 1949 reissue used sepia tone, as in the original release, beginning with the 1955 re-issue, and continuing until the film's 50th anniversary <a href="/wiki/VHS" title="VHS">VHS</a> release in 1989, these opening Kansas sequences were shown in black and white instead of the sepia tone as originally printed. (This includes television showings.)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>The MGM "Children's Matinees" series rereleased the film twice, in both 1970 and 1971.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> It was for this release that the film received a G rating from the MPAA.</p>
<p>For the film's then-upcoming 60th anniversary, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Warner_Bros._Pictures" title="Warner Bros. Pictures">Warner Bros. Pictures</a> released a "Special Edition" on November 6, 1998, digitally restored with remastered audio.</p>
<p>In 2002, the film had a <a href="/wiki/Limited_release" title="Limited release">very limited re-release</a> in U.S. theaters, earning only $139,905.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<p>On September 23, 2009, the film was rereleased in select theaters for a one-night-only event in honor of its 70th anniversary and as a promotion for various new disc releases later in the month. An encore of this event was released in theaters on November 17, 2009.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup></p>
<p>An <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/IMAX_3D" title="IMAX 3D">IMAX 3D</a> theatrical re-release played at 300 theaters in North America for one week only beginning September 20, 2013, as part of the film's 75th anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMAX_3D_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-IMAX_3D-58">[58]</a></sup> Warner Bros. spent $25 million on advertising. The studio hosted a premiere of the film's first IMAX 3D release on September 15, 2013, from the newly remodeled <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/TCL_Chinese_Theatre" title="TCL Chinese Theatre">TCL Chinese Theatre</a> (formerly Grauman's Chinese Theatre, the site of the film's Hollywood premiere) in Hollywood. It was the first to play at the new theater and served as the grand opening of Hollywood's first 3D IMAX screen. It was also shown as a special presentation at the <a href="/wiki/2013_Toronto_International_Film_Festival" title="2013 Toronto International Film Festival">2013 Toronto International Film Festival</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This re-release grossed $5.6 million at the North American box office.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2013, in preparation for its IMAX 3D release, the film was submitted again to the MPAA for re-classification. According to MPAA rules, a film that has been altered in any way from its original version must be submitted for re-classification, as the 3-D conversion fell within that guideline. Surprisingly, the 3D version received a PG rating for "Some scary moments", although no change was made to the film's original story content. The 2D version still retains its G rating.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film was rereleased on January 11 and 14, 2015, as part of the "TCM Presents" series by <a href="/wiki/Turner_Classic_Movies" title="Turner Classic Movies">Turner Classic Movies</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Television">Television</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Television">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/The_Wizard_of_Oz_on_television" title="The Wizard of Oz on television">The Wizard of Oz on television</a></div>
<p>The film was first shown on television on November 3, 1956, by <a href="/wiki/CBS" title="CBS">CBS</a>, as the last installment of the <i><a href="/wiki/Ford_Star_Jubilee" title="Ford Star Jubilee">Ford Star Jubilee</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Home_media">Home media</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Home media">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The film was among the first videocassettes (on both <a href="/wiki/VHS" title="VHS">VHS</a> and <a href="/wiki/Betamax" title="Betamax">Betamax</a> format for the 1980 release) by <a href="/wiki/MGM_Home_Entertainment" title="MGM Home Entertainment">MGM/CBS Home Video</a> in 1980;<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> all current home video releases are by <a href="/wiki/Warner_Home_Video" title="Warner Home Video">Warner Home Video</a> (via current rights holder <a href="/wiki/Turner_Entertainment" title="Turner Entertainment">Turner Entertainment</a>). The first <a href="/wiki/LaserDisc" title="LaserDisc">LaserDisc</a> release of it was in 1982, with two versions of a second (one from Turner and one from <a href="/wiki/The_Criterion_Collection" title="The Criterion Collection">The Criterion Collection</a> with a commentary track) for the 50th anniversary release in 1989, a third in 1991, a fourth in 1993, a fifth in 1995 and a sixth and final LaserDisc release on September 11, 1996.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>Prior to the wide-home-video release in 1980, the film was also released multiple times for the home-video commercial market (on a limited scale) on <a href="/wiki/Super_8_film" title="Super 8 film">Super 8 film</a> (<a href="/wiki/8_mm_film" title="8 mm film">8 mm</a> format) during the 1970s. These releases include an edited English version (roughly 10 minutes, and roughly 20 minutes), as well as edited Spanish versions of the classic. Also, a full commercial release of it was made on Super 8 (on multiple reels) that came out in the 1970s, as well, for the commercial market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition to VHS (and later, LaserDisc), the film has been released multiple times during the 1980s on the Betamax format, beginning in 1980 simultaneously with the VHS release.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup></p>
<p>The film was released for the first and only time on the <a href="/wiki/Capacitance_Electronic_Disc" title="Capacitance Electronic Disc">CED</a> format in 1982 by MGM/UA Home Video.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>Outside of the North American and European markets, the film has also been released multiple times on the <a href="/wiki/Video_CD" title="Video CD">Video CD</a> format since the 1990s in Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup></p>
<p>The first DVD release was on March 26, 1997, by MGM/Turner and contained no special features or supplements. It was re-released by Warner Bros. for its 60th anniversary on October 19, 1999, with its soundtrack presented in a new 5.1 <a href="/wiki/Surround_sound" title="Surround sound">surround sound</a> mix. The monochrome-to-color transition was more smoothly accomplished by digitally keeping the inside of the house in monochrome while Dorothy and the reveal of Munchkinland are in color. <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (April 2013)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The DVD also contained a behind-the-scenes documentary, <i>The Wonderful Wizard of Oz: The Making of a Movie Classic</i>, produced in 1990 and hosted by <a href="/wiki/Angela_Lansbury" title="Angela Lansbury">Angela Lansbury</a>, which was originally shown on television immediately following the 1990 telecast of the film; it had been featured in the 1993 "Ultimate Oz" LaserDisc release. Outtakes, the deleted "Jitterbug" musical number, clips of pre-1939 <i>Oz</i> adaptations, trailers, newsreels, and a portrait gallery were also included, as well as two radio programs of the era publicizing the film.</p>
<p>In 2005, two DVD editions were released, both featuring a newly restored version of the film with an <a href="/wiki/Audio_commentary" title="Audio commentary">audio commentary</a> and an isolated music and effects track. One of the two DVD releases was a "Two-Disc Special Edition", featuring production documentaries, trailers, various outtakes, newsreels, radio shows and still galleries. The other set, a "Three-Disc Collector's Edition", included these features, as well as the digitally restored 80th-anniversary edition of the 1925 feature-length silent film version of <i>The Wizard of Oz</i>, other silent <i>Oz</i> adaptations and a 1933 animated short version.</p>
<p>The film was released on <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray</a> on September 29, 2009, for its 70th anniversary in a four-disc "Ultimate Collector's Edition", including all the bonus features from the 2005 Collector's Edition DVD, new bonus features about Victor Fleming and the surviving Munchkins, the telefilm <i><a href="/wiki/The_Dreamer_of_Oz:_The_L._Frank_Baum_Story" title="The Dreamer of Oz: The L. Frank Baum Story">The Dreamer of Oz: The L. Frank Baum Story</a></i>, and the miniseries <i>MGM: When the Lion Roars</i>. For this edition, Warner commissioned a new transfer at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8K_Video_Format" title="8K Video Format">8K resolution</a> from the original film negatives. The restoration job was given to Prime Focus World.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> This restored version also features a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lossless" title="Lossless">lossless</a> 5.1 <a href="/wiki/Dolby_TrueHD" title="Dolby TrueHD">Dolby TrueHD</a> audio track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> A DVD version was also released as a Two-Disc Special Edition and a Five-Disc Ultimate Collector's Edition.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2013)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>On December 1, 2009,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> three Blu-ray discs of the Ultimate Collector's Edition were repackaged as a less expensive "Emerald Edition", with an Emerald Edition four-disc DVD arriving the following week. A single-disc Blu-ray, containing the restored movie and all the extra features of the two-disc Special Edition DVD, also became available on March 16, 2010.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (October 2011)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup></p>
<p>In 2013, the film was re-released on DVD, Blu-ray, <a href="/wiki/Blu-ray#Blu-ray_3D" title="Blu-ray">Blu-ray 3D</a> and <a href="/wiki/UltraViolet_(system)" title="UltraViolet (system)">UltraViolet</a> for the 90th anniversary of <a href="/wiki/Warner_Bros." title="Warner Bros.">Warner Bros.</a> and as part of its 75th anniversary.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IMAX_3D_58-1"><a href="#cite_note-IMAX_3D-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup></p>
<p>Also, multiple special editions were released in celebration of the film's the 75th anniversary in 2013, exclusively by both <a href="/wiki/Best_Buy" title="Best Buy">Best Buy</a> (a SteelBook of the 3D Blu-ray) and another version that came with a keepsake lunch bag released by <a href="/wiki/Target_Corporation" title="Target Corporation">Target</a> stores.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Awards_and_honors">Awards and honors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=The_Wizard_of_Oz_(1939_film)&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Awards and honors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
